### 3D-printed upper limb prostheses a review.pdf

**Figures**

- **Figure 1** (p. 3): Robohand: the hand that was the inspiration for the development of the global community. Image reproduced with permission from Brod Marsh on behalf of Robohand Australia see http://www.robohand.net/wp-content/uploads/ 2013/03/2013-03-29-11-10-58-b.jpg, i.e., the copyright holder.
- **Figure 2** (p. 5): Examples of 3D-printed upper limb prostheses (a) Andrianesis’ Hand: an externally powered forearm prosthesis. Andrianesis and Tzes [1], with permission of Springer. (b) Gosselin’s hand: a body powered forearm prosthesis. Original source: Laliberte et al. No changes made. Reproduced under creative commons attribution 3.0 [3]. (c) Cyborg beast: a bod
- **Figure 3** (p. 5): Almost two-thirds of the devices are forearm prostheses from which slightly more than half of the devices are externally powered.
- **Figure 4** (p. 6): Slightly more than half of all the prostheses are body powered from which slightly more than half of the devices are controlled by the wrist.
- **Figure 5** (p. 6): More than half of the actuated hands have an equal force between the fingers. Slightly more than a third of the hands have fingers with independ- ent force due to a separate motor for each finger. Only a small number of the hands have a mechanical linkage system to distribute the force along the fingers.
- **Figure 6** (p. 7): The type of flexor used to close the prosthesis. The majority of the hands use cables or cords to close the hand. The remainder of the hands use mechanical linkages and one uses compressed air.
- **Figure 7** (p. 7): The type of extensor used to open the prosthesis. Almost two-thirds of the hands use elasticity in the form of elastic cords or bands or compliant mech- anisms to open the hand automatically.
- **Figure 8** (p. 11): The various fabrication methods used to print the prostheses. The majority of the devices are made using FDM technology

**Tables**

- **Table 1** (p. 4): General specifications and mechanical specifications of the 3D-printed hand prostheses.
- **Table 2** (p. 6): Weight of the 3D-printed hand prostheses. Only 11 of the 58 3D-printed upper limb prostheses specified their weights.
- **Table 3** (p. 8): Kinematic specifications of the 3D-printed hand prostheses.
- **Table 4** (p. 10): Print specifications of the 3D-printed hand prostheses. The first part of the table shows the 3D-printed upper limb prostheses found in scientific literature and the second part of the table shows the devices found with the internet search.

- **FIGURE 5** (p. 7): 3D-Printed prosthetic hand and socket fully assembled parts. (a) mechanical components, (b) electrical components, and (c) battery and Raspberry Pi compartment electrical components.
- **FIGURE 6** (p. 8): Complete algorithm selection flow diagram. Left: Model training using EMG signals. Right: Algorithm’s parameter tuning while using the prosthesis.
- **FIGURE 7** (p. 8): State machine for prosthetic hand control. The system transitions between a default state (Extended Hand) and five active gestures (Clamp, Cylinder 1, Cylinder 2, Handle, and Typing). Flexion movements switch from resting to active states, while extension movements return the hand to the resting position or transition to the next gesture.
- **FIGURE 8** (p. 9): Sequential protocol for prosthesis fitting, AI training, and functional assessment. (a) 3D scanning and anthropometric measurements of the residual limb and the contralateral hand, (b) initial prosthetic socket fitting and sEMG data acquisition for AI-based control training, with the volunteer executing predefined movements while wearing the prosth
- **FIGURE 9** (p. 10): Confusion matrices for each user for the XGBoost trained models, using a window size of 150ms and 200ms, (a) models using the two-layer stacked architecture, (b) a multiclass classification approach targeting three distinct classes.

### Evaluating clearance parameters of 3D printed joints for the automated design of a non-assembly delta robot.pdf

**Figures**

- **Figure 1** (p. 2): Parametrized revolute (I) and spherical joint (III) with proposed optimized design concepts for non-assembly production (II, IV). Both concepts for the new design of printable non-assembly joints target on the one hand a reduction of the direct contact surface of the parts moving with respect to each other and on the other hand an improved possibil
- **Figure 2** (p. 3): Parameters defining the delta robot kinematic: Length of upper (1) and lower (2) arm, side length of triangle defined by intersections of rotation axis of revolute joints at base (3) and end effector (4). Using spherical joints (a), revolute joints (b) and a herringbone teeth gear (c) for creating a delta robot.
- **Figure 3** (p. 3): With the help of automated design, suitable mechanisms can be created with more abstract, application-specific information. In this case, for example, a matching delta kinematics can be created from the specifications of the workspace.
- **Figure 4** (p. 4): Test setup in order to find the optimum clearance dimensions for the joints. The joint (1) is fixed in a lever press (4), the force required to release the joint Fr is measured with a force (2)/displacement (3) measurement system.
- **Figure 5** (p. 4): Experimental results from finding the minimum clearance dimensions of revolute joints (I), revolute joints with minimized contact area (II), ball joints (III) and ball joints with minimized contact area (IV). Each marker in Ia)-IVa) represents a test series of n = 3.
- **Figure 6** (p. 5): Test setup in order to test the non-assembly delta robot: The movement of the delta robot (1) is measured with an optical tracker at the tip (2) and at the fixed motor housing (3). With a stereo camera, position and orientation of the end effector can be measured.
- **Figure 7** (p. 6): Verification of the functionality of the delta by following a cube and a cylinder pattern: The blue markers (x) correspond to the given points, while the red markers (o) show to the actual measured end effector positions.

### Advancements, Trends and Future Prospects of Lower Limb Prosthesis.pdf

**Figures**

- **FIGURE 1** (p. 2): Common causes of lower limb amputations that happened in life routine matters have been illustrated. We observed the traumatic injuries, different diseases, and some multifarious as depicted in the Figure 1.
- **FIGURE 2** (p. 5): Lower limb prosthesis are classified as passive, active, and semi-active keeping in view the actuation power.
- **FIGURE 3** (p. 7): This figure shows images of commercially available passive, active, and semi-active prostheses from left to right. All the images have been combined into one picture and the sources have been cited in the description given in the section of ‘‘commercial development in lower limb prosthesis.’’
- **FIGURE 4** (p. 14): This figure shows the count of patents in lower limb prostheses as per inclusion and exclusion criteria. Patents studied were 105, 131, 228, and 11 for prosthesis related work for foot, ankle, knee and hip respectively.
- **FIGURE 5** (p. 14): The histogram shows the patents published for the design of the foot, ankle, knee, and hip. Design and accessories like liners have been considered in our open source database FAKH-50 which involves patents from 1970 to 2020.

- **Fig 21** (p. 17): OpenSCAD sample codes to show the effect of its functional programming paradigm. (A) Assignment in line 1 has no effect since variables keep a constant value during their entire lifetime. (B) The code shows an invalid assignment since variables cannot change their values.
- **Fig 22** (p. 17): Sample codes to show the variable scope in OpenSCAD. (A) Assignment in line 4 has no effect outside the if statement; therefore the sphere in line 7 will have a radius of 1. (B) This code produces the same result as in (A) even that the variable assignments are placed after the sphere function calls that use those variables.
- **Fig 23** (p. 18): Parametric design and variable scope in OpenSCAD. Code (A) will not work because h has not been defined outside the if statement. Codes (B) and (C) will work.
- **Fig 24** (p. 19): Sample codes of dictionaries for defining component dimensions in Python. (A) Dictionary DIN125_H defines the height (thickness) of some DIN 125 washers. Keys can be a float number, as in line 2. Line 6 shows how to obtain the height (0.5) of a DIN125 M2.5 washer from the dictionary. (B) Dictionaries can be multidimensional and can have strings as 
- **Fig 25** (p. 21): Source codes and their generated models. (A) OpenSCAD can export to polygonal mesh models, but not export to parametric models. (B) FreeCAD can export to both polygonal mesh models and standard parametric models.
- **Fig 26** (p. 24): Mesh generation times in OpenSCAD an FreeCAD.

- **Fig 5** (p. 7): Exploded view of the belt tensioner. The two printable parts are drawn in colors: tensioner holder (blue) and idler tensioner (orange).

- **Fig 7** (p. 8): 3D printing orientation of the four printable models to avoid supports.

### THE DESIGN OF AN UPPER-LIMB PROSTHETIC FINGER USING ALGORITHMS FOR FULL CUSTOMIZATION.pdf

**Figures**

- **Figure 1** (p. 2): Considered sections of finger dimensions marked with L and t for length and breadth [10]

### J. T. ten Kate - 3D-printed upper limb prostheses a review [2017].pdf

**Figures**

- **Figure 1** (p. 3): Robohand: the hand that was the inspiration for the development of the global community. Image reproduced with permission from Brod Marsh on behalf of Robohand Australia see http://www.robohand.net/wp-content/uploads/ 2013/03/2013-03-29-11-10-58-b.jpg, i.e., the copyright holder.
- **Figure 2** (p. 5): Examples of 3D-printed upper limb prostheses (a) Andrianesis’ Hand: an externally powered forearm prosthesis. Andrianesis and Tzes [1], with permission of Springer. (b) Gosselin’s hand: a body powered forearm prosthesis. Original source: Laliberte et al. No changes made. Reproduced under creative commons attribution 3.0 [3]. (c) Cyborg beast: a bod
- **Figure 3** (p. 5): Almost two-thirds of the devices are forearm prostheses from which slightly more than half of the devices are externally powered.
- **Figure 4** (p. 6): Slightly more than half of all the prostheses are body powered from which slightly more than half of the devices are controlled by the wrist.
- **Figure 5** (p. 6): More than half of the actuated hands have an equal force between the fingers. Slightly more than a third of the hands have fingers with independ- ent force due to a separate motor for each finger. Only a small number of the hands have a mechanical linkage system to distribute the force along the fingers.
- **Figure 6** (p. 7): The type of flexor used to close the prosthesis. The majority of the hands use cables or cords to close the hand. The remainder of the hands use mechanical linkages and one uses compressed air.
- **Figure 7** (p. 7): The type of extensor used to open the prosthesis. Almost two-thirds of the hands use elasticity in the form of elastic cords or bands or compliant mech- anisms to open the hand automatically.
- **Figure 8** (p. 11): The various fabrication methods used to print the prostheses. The majority of the devices are made using FDM technology

**Tables**

- **Table 1** (p. 4): General specifications and mechanical specifications of the 3D-printed hand prostheses.
- **Table 2** (p. 6): Weight of the 3D-printed hand prostheses. Only 11 of the 58 3D-printed upper limb prostheses specified their weights.
- **Table 3** (p. 8): Kinematic specifications of the 3D-printed hand prostheses.
- **Table 4** (p. 10): Print specifications of the 3D-printed hand prostheses. The first part of the table shows the 3D-printed upper limb prostheses found in scientific literature and the second part of the table shows the devices found with the internet search.

### cross-sectional International Multicenter Study on Quality of Life and Reasons for Abandonment of Upper Limb Prostheses.pdf

**Figures**

- **Figure 1** (p. 4): Reported stump problems. The most common symptom of the stump was tingling (51%, n = 88), followed by phantom pain (45%, n = 79), pain in the stump (24%, n = 41), itching (13%, n = 22), skin problems (6%, n = 10), and others (6%, n = 11). Twenty percent of patients (n=34) responded that they had no stump problem. Chubu, Chubu Rosai Hospital; Hyogo,
- **Figure 2** (p. 4): Main prosthetic type used. The greatest number of prosthesis users employed a myoelectric prosthesis (n = 75). There were also 50 body-powered prosthesis users and 33 cosmetic prosthetic users. Chubu, Chubu Rosai Hospital; Hyogo, Hyogo Rehabilitation Center Central Hospital; NRCD, National Rehabilitation Center for Persons with Disabilities; UM, Un
- **Figure 3** (p. 4): Days per week that patients used a prosthesis. One hundred eight patients (70%) used their prosthesis every day. Chubu, Chubu Rosai Hospital; Hyogo, Hyogo Rehabilitation Center Central Hospi- tal; NRCD, National Rehabilitation Center for Persons with Disabili- ties; UM, University of Michigan.
- **Figure 4** (p. 5): Time per day that patients used a prosthesis. One hundred patients (67%) wore their prosthesis more than 8 h/d. Chubu, Chubu Rosai Hospital; Hyogo, Hyogo Rehabilitation Center Central Hospi- tal; NRCD, National Rehabilitation Center for Persons with Disabili- ties; UM, University of Michigan.
- **Figure 5** (p. 5): Activities for which patients used a prosthesis. Patients used their prosthesis for leisure or outings (n = 104), job (n = 97), driving (n = 73), cooking and eating (n = 49), sports (n = 18), writing (n = 15), and other activities (n = 27). Chubu, Chubu Rosai Hospital; Hyogo, Hyogo Rehabilitation Center Central Hospital; NRCD, National Re- habilita
- **Figure 6** (p. 5): Satisfaction with the prosthesis. Twelve patients (8%) report- ed being very satisfied with their prosthesis, 76 patients (51%) were satisfied, and 38 patients (25%) were neutral. Twenty-three patients (15%) were dissatisfied, and 1 patient was very dissatisfied with their prosthesis. Chubu, Chubu Rosai Hospital; Hyogo, Hyogo Rehabilita- tion Cente
- **Figure 7** (p. 7): EQ-5D utility score according to the level of limb absence. EQ-5D utility score did not significantly differ according to the level of upper limb deficiency.
